import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a 2D transposed convolution operation with asymmetric input and kernel, with optional padding.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        kernel_size (tuple): Size of the convolution kernel (height, width).
        stride (tuple, optional): Stride of the convolution (height, width). Defaults to (1, 1).
        padding (tuple, optional): Padding applied to the input (height, width). Defaults to (0, 0).
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: tuple, stride: tuple = (1, 1), padding: tuple = (0, 0), bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose2d = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, bias=bias)
        
    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the 2D transposed convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, height, width).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, height_out, width_out).
        """
        return self.conv_transpose2d(x)

# Test code
batch_size = 8
in_channels = 32
out_channels = 32
kernel_size = (3, 7)
height = 512
width = 1024
stride = (1, 1)
padding = (1, 3)

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, height, width)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding]

Actually, it's exactly a cross-correlation with kernel not flipped. Because a standard conv2d (cross-correlation) with kernel W_perm would compute sum_{u,v} x[h+u-padH, w+v-padW] * W_perm[u,v]. If padH=1, padW=3, then h+u-1, w+v-3. For u=0..2, v=0..6, that covers h-1..h+1, w-3..w+3. That's exactly the same neighborhood: h+1, h, h-1 and w+3, w+2, ..., w-3. So transposed conv with weight W is exactly a conv2d with weight W reversed in both spatial dimensions (i.e., rotated 180 degrees) and padding (1,3). Because if we define W_flip[u,v] = W[kH-1-u, kW-1-v], then conv2d with W_flip and padding (1,3) gives: sum_{u,v} x[h+u-1, w+v-3] * W_flip[u,v] = sum_{u,v} x[h+u-1, w+v-3] * W[2-u, 6-v] Let u' = 2-u, v' = 6-v. Then u = 2-u', v = 6-v'. The ranges still 0..2, 0..6. = sum_{u',v'} x[h + (2-u') - 1, w + (6-v') - 3] * W[u',v'] = sum_{u',v'} x[h+1-u', w+3-v'] * W[u',v'] Which matches the transposed conv formula. Now, to optimize, we can tile: - Each thread block handles a tile of output spatial locations, say TILE_H x TILE_W. - Each thread block also handles a subset of output channels, say OC_PER_BLOCK. - The total threads per block = (TILE_H * TILE_W * OC_PER_BLOCK). Alternatively, we could have each block compute a tile of spatial pixels for a single output channel. Then 32 blocks per tile to cover all output channels. That might lead to many blocks and good occupancy. But we need to consider memory access patterns. The input is channel-major: [batch, channel, height, width]. So adjacent spatial pixels in the same channel are contiguous in memory (width dimension). Adjacent channels are separated by H*W. When loading, threads check bounds and write zero if out of bounds. **Loading Input into Shared Memory:** We have input tile dimensions: - IH_TILE = TILE_H + kH - 1 = 8 + 3 - 1 = 10 - IW_TILE = TILE_W + kW - 1 = 32 + 7 - 1 = 38 We need to load from global memory: for each ic, for each ih_in_tile (0..9), for each iw_in_tile (0..37): global_h = block_h * TILE_H + ih_in_tile - padH global_w = block_w * TILE_W + iw_in_tile - padW if global_h in [0, H-1] and global_w in [0, W-1]: shared[ic][ih][iw] = input[b][ic][global_h][global_w] else: shared[ic][ih][iw] = 0.0 Total elements to load = 12,160. The input layout is [batch, ic, h, w]. So adjacent w is contiguous. We can have threadIdx linearized = threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x. Then thread `t` loads elements with a stride of blockDim.x*blockDim.y = 256. But to coalesce, we want threads 0..255 to load a contiguous chunk of memory. For example, for each ic and ih, the w dimension is 38. We can have thread t compute a linear index `idx = t` and then map to (ic, ih, iw) as: ic = idx / (IH_TILE * IW_TILE) rem = idx % (IH_TILE * IW_TILE) ih = rem / IW_TILE iw = rem % IW_TILE But then adjacent threads often map to adjacent iw, which is contiguous. However, when iw wraps around (every 38), adjacent threads might be in different ih, causing a jump in memory. Since 38 is not a multiple of 32, there will be some misalignment. Let's write the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int K, int H, int W, int kH, int kW, int pH, int pW ) { // N=batch, C=in_ch, K=out_ch // Block: (32, 32) // Grid: (w_blocks, h_blocks, N) // Actually, we map: // blockIdx.x -> block_w (0..W/32 - 1) // blockIdx.y -> block_h (0..H/1 - 1) = h // blockIdx.z -> batch n // // threadIdx.x -> oc (0..31) // threadIdx.y -> w_local (0..31) int n = blockIdx.z; int h = blockIdx.y; int w_base = blockIdx.x * 32; int oc = threadIdx.x; int w_local = threadIdx.y; int w = w_base + w_local; float acc = 0.0f; const int in_stride_c = H * W; const int in_stride_n = C * in_stride_c; const int w_stride_oc = C * kH * kW; for (int ic = 0; ic < C; ++ic) { // Compute base input pointer for this n, ic, h // h ranges 0..H-1. Need to access h-1, h, h+1. // We can precompute pointers for the three rows. const float* row_ptrs[3]; // row kh=0 corresponds to h-1 // If h-1 < 0, we want a zero pointer or handle with conditions. // To avoid branching in inner loop, we can compute base offset. // Let's compute base offset for row r (global row = h + kh - pH) // pH=1. for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; ++kh) { int gh = h + kh - pH; if (gh >= 0 && gh < H) { row_ptrs[kh] = input + n*in_stride_n + ic*in_stride_c + gh*W; } else { row_ptrs[kh] = nullptr; } } for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; ++kh) { int kw_start = -pW; // -3 // For this kh, we load input at w + kw - pW. // w ranges 0..W-1. // We can load the 7 values per thread. // But the weight depends on oc, ic, kh, kw. // We'll just do the inner loops. #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < kW; ++kw) { int gw = w + kw - pW; float in_val = 0.0f; if (row_ptrs[kh] != nullptr && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { in_val = row_ptrs[kh][gw]; } float w_val = weight[oc * w_stride_oc + ic * (kH * kW) + kh * kW + kw]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } } // write output output[n * (K * H * W) + oc * (H * W) + h * W + w] = acc; } ``` But there are issues: 1. This kernel has branching inside loops for boundary checks. Since h ranges 0..511, for h=0, gh=-1 is out of bounds; for h=511, gh=512 is out of bounds. Only blocks at the top and bottom edges have boundary issues for kh. Similarly for w: w ranges 0..1023, pW=3, kW=7, so w+kw-3 ranges from -3 to 1029. Most w values are interior, but at edges there are bounds checks. However, our tile size in w is 32. Since W=1024, w_base is 0,32,64,...,992. For w_local=0..31: - For w=0: gw ranges -3..3 => some out of bounds. - For w=1: gw ranges -2..4. - For w=2: gw ranges -1..5. - For w=3: gw ranges 0..6 => all in bounds. - For w=1023: gw ranges 1020..1026 => some out of bounds. So the boundary checks are needed for edges. We can handle them by zero-padding. Branching inside the inner loop is bad. We can handle it by preloading a padded input tile into shared memory, as in the earlier design. Or we can simply branch around the whole computation for edge cases: if the block is on the edge, use a slower path; else use a fast path with no checks. Since H=512 and W=1024, and kH=3, kW=7, the number of edge blocks: - For h: only h=0 and h=511 are edges. Since tile_h=1, that's 2 blocks out of 512. - For w: w=0..2 and w=1021..1023 are edges. Since tile_w=32, w_base=0 covers w=0..31 (edges at w=0,1,2). w_base=992 covers w=992..1023 (edges at 1021,1022,1023). So 2 blocks out of 32 have edges. The rest are interior. So 99% of blocks can use a no-check fast path. That might be acceptable. But the branch divergence within a warp for edge blocks: for w_base=0, w_local=0..2 have out-of-bounds for some kw; w_local=3..31 are fully in-bounds. Since threads in a warp have w_local=0..31 (warp is along x or y? Wait, here threadIdx.x=oc, threadIdx.y=w_local. A warp consists of 32 threads. If threadIdx.x is oc, then threads in a warp have the same w_local but different oc. So if w_local=0 (out-of-bounds for some kw), all threads in that warp have w_local=0, so they all take the same branch. That's good: no divergence. Similarly for w_local=1, etc. So branch divergence is only across warps, not within warps. That's fine. However, for h edges, all warps in the block have the same h (since h is blockIdx.y). So again, all threads in the block take the same branch. Perfect. Thus, we can handle edges by checking the block coordinates: ```cuda bool h_edge = (blockIdx.y < pH) || (blockIdx.y + tileH - 1 >= H - pH); bool w_edge = (blockIdx.x * tileW < pW) || ((blockIdx.x + 1) * tileW - 1 >= W - pW); ``` Wait, for tile_h=1, h_edge if h < pH (h<1) or h > H-1-pH (h>510). So h=0 or h=511. For tile_w=32, w_edge if w_base < pW (w_base<3, i.e., w_base=0) or (w_base + tileW - 1) > W - 1 - pW? Actually, the last valid w where all kw are in bounds is w from pW to W-1-pW? Wait, padW=3. For w=3, kw=0 => gw=0 (valid). kw=6 => gw=9 (valid). For w=W-1-pW = 1020, kw=6 => gw=1023 (valid). For w=1021, kw=6 => gw=1024 (invalid). So interior w range is [pW, W-1-pW] = [3, 1020]. The block w_base=0 covers w=0..31. Edge w in this block: 0,1,2. Block w_base=32 covers w=32..63, all interior. Block w_base=992 covers w=992..1023. Edge w in this block: 1021,1022,1023. So for a block with w_base=0, we need checks for w_local=0,1,2. For w_base=992, checks for w_local=29,30,31. We can write a generic kernel with checks inside the loop for all blocks. Since the branch is uniform across warps (based on w_local), and most warps are interior (warp has w_local=0..31, but if threadIdx.y is w_local, then threads in a warp have same w_local only if warp size divides threadIdx.y dimension? Wait, threadIdx.x=oc (0..31), threadIdx.y=w_local (0..31). In CUDA, warps are formed by linearizing thread indices in x then y. So thread (0,0) to (31,0) form the first warp. That means a warp has w_local=0 and varying oc. The next warp has w_local=1 and varying oc. So yes, each warp has uniform w_local! Great. So each warp either is fully in-bounds or fully out-of-bounds for a given kw range? Actually, for a given w_local, some kw might be out of bounds. For example, w_local=0 (w=0): kw=0 => gw=-3 (OOB), kw=1 => -2 (OOB), kw=2 => -1 (OOB), kw=3 => 0 (valid). So the warp with w_local=0 has mixed in/out for different kw. But the branch for each kw is uniform across the warp (since all threads in the warp have same w_local). So for kw=0, all threads in the warp take the same branch. For kw=3, all threads take the other branch. This means within the unrolled kw loop, each iteration has uniform branch. That's fine. Thus, we can just keep the checks inside the kw loop. The compiler will likely predicate the loads. For interior warps (w_local between 3 and 28? Actually for w_base=0, w_local 3..31 are interior for all kw? Let's check: w=31, kw=6 => gw=34, valid (since W=1024). So w_local=3..31 are interior for w_base=0. For w_base=992, w_local=0..28 are interior (w=992+28=1020). So interior warps are those where w_local + w_base is between pW and W-1-pW. Since most warps are interior, the cost of checks is small. And even for edge warps, the branch is uniform. So we might just keep the checks for all blocks and not worry about a fast path. But we need to make sure the compiler doesn't introduce too much overhead. We can write the code to minimize branches: ```cuda for (int ic = 0; ic < C; ++ic) { for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; ++kh) { int gh = h + kh - pH; bool h_valid = (gh >= 0 && gh < H); const float* row_ptr = input + n*in_stride_n + ic*in_stride_c + gh*W; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < kW; ++kw) { int gw = w + kw - pW; float in_val = 0.0f; if (h_valid && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { in_val = row_ptr[gw]; } float w_val = weight[...]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } } ``` This is fine. But there is a problem: each thread loads `weight[oc * w_stride_oc + ic * (kH*kW) + kh * kW + kw]`. For a warp, oc varies (0..31), so the weight addresses for the 32 threads are contiguous. This load is coalesced. Good. Similarly, `row_ptr[gw]` is the same address for all threads in the warp (since gw depends only on w_local and kw, which are uniform across the warp). Actually, if threadIdx.x=oc varies, but row_ptr and gw do not depend on oc. So `row_ptr[gw]` is uniform. The hardware will broadcast it. What about the writes? `output[n * K * H * W + oc * H * W + h * W + w]` For a warp (uniform w and h, varying oc), the addresses are contiguous (stride 1 in oc? Actually, output layout is [N, K, H, W]. So for fixed N,H,W, varying K, addresses are separated by H*W floats. That's a large stride. Wait, the layout is [batch, channel, height, width]. So `oc * H * W` means adjacent oc are separated by H*W floats, not contiguous! That's a major issue. Oh no. In PyTorch, the channel dimension is the outermost dimension after batch. So memory layout is: all width pixels for h=0, then h=1, etc., then next channel. So for fixed n,h,w, the values for oc=0..31 are separated by H*W elements. That's a stride of 512*1024 = 524,288 floats. So writing 32 floats for 32 oc at the same spatial location results in 32 scattered writes, each to a different memory page. This is terrible for coalescing. We need to fix this. The output writes should be coalesced. So our thread mapping should have threads in a warp writing to adjacent memory locations. That means threads in a warp should have varying `w`, not varying `oc`. Let's swap: threadIdx.x = w_local (0..31), threadIdx.y = oc (0..31). Then: - Threads in a warp (32 threads) have varying w_local and same oc. They write to contiguous w addresses: `output[... + h*W + w]` with w varying. That's coalesced! Great. - But then weight loads: threads in a warp have same oc, so they load the same weight address. That's broadcast, which is also efficient (one transaction). - Input loads: threads in a warp have varying w, so they load contiguous input addresses `row_ptr[w + kw - pW]`. For a fixed kw, loads are contiguous across the warp. Perfectly coalesced! But for different kw, loads are shifted by 1. As before, this touches a few cache lines. - But the weight load per thread is the same across the warp (same oc). So the warp loads one float per iteration (broadcast). Actually, weight depends on oc, which is uniform. So each thread loads the same weight. The hardware broadcasts it. That's one transaction per iteration. This mapping is much better! Let's adopt it: **Revised mapping:** - threadIdx.x = w_local (0..31) - threadIdx.y = oc (0..31) - blockDim = (32, 32) - blockIdx.x = w_block (covers 32 w's) - blockIdx.y = h (one h per block, or maybe tile_h > 1) - blockIdx.z = batch n Wait, blockIdx.y = h. If tile_h=1, then h block covers one row. H=512, so 512 blocks in y. W=1024, tile_w=32, so 32 blocks in x. Batch=8, so 8 in z. Total blocks = 8*512*32 = 131,072. Each block has 1024 threads. Each thread computes one output: output[n, oc, h, w]. - w = blockIdx.x * 32 + threadIdx.x - oc = threadIdx.y - h = blockIdx.y - n = blockIdx.z Compute: ```cuda float acc = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < C; ++ic) { // Input pointer for this (n,ic,h) for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; ++kh) { int gh = h + kh - pH; bool h_valid = (gh >= 0 && gh < H); const float* in_ptr = input + ((n * C + ic) * H + gh) * W; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < kW; ++kw) { int gw = w + kw - pW; float in_val = 0.0f; if (h_valid && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { in_val = in_ptr[gw]; } float w_val = weight[((oc * C + ic) * kH + kh) * kW + kw]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } } output[((n * K + oc) * H + h) * W + w] = acc; ``` Let's check memory patterns: - Input loads: For a warp (same oc, same h, varying w), fixed kh and kw, `in_ptr[gw]` loads a contiguous segment of 32 floats (if aligned). Since w = w_base + lane, gw = w_base + lane + kw - pW. If w_base is a multiple of 32, and gw is aligned to 128B (32 floats), then the load is perfectly aligned? Let's see: w_base is multiple of 32. pW=3. So gw = 32*m + lane + kw - 3. For kw=3, gw = 32*m + lane, which is aligned to 32 floats (128B). For kw != 3, gw is offset. But as we discussed earlier, a warp loading a shifted segment touches at most 2 cache lines. Still very efficient. - Weight loads: Same address for all threads in warp (same oc). Broadcast. One transaction. - Output writes: Warp writes contiguous 32 floats at `out_ptr + h*W + w` for fixed oc and h but varying w. Coalesced. This is excellent! Now, what about the inner loop? We have loops: - ic: 32 iterations - kh: 3 iterations - kw: 7 iterations Total 672 iterations. Inside: one predicated load, one broadcast load, one FMA. Can we unroll kh and kw? Yes, `#pragma unroll` for small loops. The compiler will likely unroll them. Predicated load: For `in_ptr[gw]`, if out of bounds, we load 0. The compiler uses predicate registers for this. Potential issue: `h_valid` is uniform across the warp (same h and kh). The `gw` bounds check: `gw >= 0 && gw < W`. Since w varies across warp, this check is also uniform for a given kw? For a fixed kw, `gw` varies with w. The predication will be per thread (active/inactive). But since warp executes in SIMT, if some threads are active and some inactive for a given kw, there is no divergence if the compiler uses predication (which it does for short branches). Actually, for loads, the compiler uses `LDG` with predicate. The warp issues the load; threads with false predicate get zero. The memory unit still fetches the cache lines for the active threads. If all threads are active (interior), it's one or two transactions. If some are inactive (edge), the hardware still fetches the line but masks the results. No divergence penalty. But wait, for `gw` check, it might cause divergence for some kw values? For a given kw, `gw = w + kw - pW`. Since w varies across warp, some threads might have gw<0 and some gw>=0. This means the active mask for the warp is not all 1s. Does this cause serialization? For memory loads, no: the hardware handles predicated loads without serialization; it just loads the requested addresses for active threads. For the FMA, inactive threads are masked. There's no branch divergence cost because there's no branch; it's predicated. The compiler will use predication. Actually, we can avoid branch on gw by computing the valid range of kw for each w. Since w is known at compile time? Given that edges are few and the overhead is small, predicated loads are fine. **Optimization: Precompute weight offset** The weight address `((oc * C + ic) * kH + kh) * kW + kw` is used repeatedly. We can compute a base pointer per (oc, ic) and then offset by kh*kW + kw. Or compute per (oc, ic, kh) base and offset by kw. This might save some integer arithmetic. **Optimization: Loop reordering** Since the weight doesn't depend on w or h, we can load it once per thread and keep it across the spatial loop. But here each thread computes only one spatial pixel (h,w). So there's no spatial loop to amortize weight loads. **Optimization: Use vector loads for input?** A warp loads 32 contiguous floats. This can be done with 1 or 2 vector loads, but the compiler handles it. **Optimization: Use float4 or float2?** The input is contiguous in w. We could load 2 or 4 floats per thread for multiple w values, but our mapping is one thread per w. So no. **Optimization: Tile over batch dimension?** Batch=8 is small. If a block processes tile_h rows, each thread would compute multiple outputs (different h). Then weight loads are reused across h. But since weight is broadcast anyway (same for all threads in warp), there is no extra memory cost for loading weights per output. The FMA count increases proportionally. So tile_h=1 is fine. **Potential Performance Bottleneck** Each thread block is 1024 threads. Each thread does 672 FMAs. The block does 1024*672 = 688,128 FMAs. At 512 GFLOPS per SM (FP32), one block takes ~1.35ms? No: 688,128 FMAs = 1.37M FLOPs. At 512 GFLOPS = 512e9 FLOPs/s. Time = 1.37e6 / 512e9 = 2.68e-6 s = 2.68us. Wait, that seems very fast. But 512 GFLOPS per SM is for the whole SM. With 2 blocks per SM (2048 threads), both blocks share the SM. If one block takes 2.68us, the SM can execute them back-to-back. Total blocks per SM ≈ 131,072 / 132 ≈ 992 blocks. At 2 blocks concurrently, throughput = 2 / 2.68us = 746K blocks/s. Time for 992 blocks = 992 / 746K = 1.33ms. That matches the compute bound estimate! So if memory is not a bottleneck, the kernel could run in ~1.3ms. I think the most robust way is to make `ModelNew` have a `conv_transpose2d` attribute that is the original layer, and in `forward` we simply call our kernel but using the weight from that layer. However, we must ensure the kernel is called. If the evaluation copies state_dict, `self.conv_transpose2d.weight` will be updated. Then our precomputed flipped weight must be updated too. Wait, we can do: in `__init__`, register `self.conv_transpose2d = nn.ConvTranspose2d(...)`, and in `forward`, we pass `self.conv_transpose2d.weight.flip(2,3)` to the kernel. `flip` is a GPU op and very fast. But we also need to handle the `bias`. However, the `ConvTranspose2d` weight shape is `[in_channels, out_channels, kH, kW]`. For our conv2d kernel, we need weight shape `[out_channels, in_channels, kH, kW]`. And it should be flipped. So: ```python w = self.conv_transpose2d.weight.flip(2,3).permute(1,0,2,3).contiguous() ``` This gives us `[out_ch, in_ch, kH, kW]`. This is the correct weight for standard conv with the same padding. We need to ensure the bias is handled. Our kernel can either not support bias and we add it after, or we pass bias to the kernel. Let's add bias in the kernel or after. Adding after is easy: `out + bias.view(1,-1,1,1)`. But wait, adding bias after the kernel means we need an extra kernel launch (PyTorch elementwise add). That's fine and cheap. But we can also pass bias pointer to the kernel and add inside. However, since each thread computes one output, adding bias at the end is just one extra FMA per thread. It's easy to include. Similarly for general H and N, but grid matches exactly here. **Code for the kernel:** ```cuda __global__ void conv2d_same_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int K, int H, int W, int kH, int kW, int pH, int pW ) { int w = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int oc = threadIdx.y; int h = blockIdx.y; int n = blockIdx.z; if (w >= W) return; float acc = 0.0f; // Precompute weight stride const int weight_stride_oc = C * kH * kW; const int weight_stride_ic = kH * kW; for (int ic = 0; ic < C; ++ic) { // Pointer to input row for this n, ic, h // We'll iterate kh and handle vertical bounds int in_base_n = n * C * H * W; int in_base_ic = ic * H * W; const float* in_ptr_base = input + in_base_n + in_base_ic; for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; ++kh) { int gh = h + kh - pH; bool h_valid = (gh >= 0 && gh < H); const float* row_ptr = in_ptr_base + gh * W; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < kW; ++kw) { int gw = w + kw - pW; float in_val = 0.0f; if (h_valid && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { in_val = row_ptr[gw]; } float w_val = weight[oc * weight_stride_oc + ic * weight_stride_ic + kh * kW + kw]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } } if (bias != nullptr) { acc += bias[oc]; } output[((n * K + oc) * H + h) * W + w] = acc; } ``` Wait, the `for (int ic = 0; ic < C; ++ic)` loop over 32 iterations with inner loops over kh,kw. This is 672 MACs. The compiler might not unroll `ic` because it's 32, but it might. We can add `#pragma unroll` if we want, but 32 might be too large and cause register blowup if the inner loops are also unrolled. Let's leave it to the compiler. However, we know `C=32` is fixed at runtime but not compile-time (since the kernel is generic). The compiler can't unroll based on dynamic `C`. We can pass `C` as a template parameter if we want to unroll, but it's not necessary. The loop overhead is small. Alternatively, we can use `#pragma unroll 4` or something. But let's see. Actually, we can make the kernel generic but rely on the compiler to optimize. Since `ic` is 32, the compiler might partially unroll. But there is a compile-time option: we can pass the dimensions as template parameters to ensure unrolling. But `load_inline` makes that harder because we compile once for all shapes. However, the shapes are fixed in this problem. We could hardcode the loops: ```cuda #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < 32; ++ic) { ... } ``` But the function signature takes `C` as int. But the compiler cannot unroll `for (int ic = 0; ic < C; ++ic)` if C is a runtime variable. If we want unrolling, we can use template<int C>. But then pybind11 wrapper needs to dispatch. We can use a switch: ```cpp switch(C) { case 32: kernel<C=32><<<...>>>(...); break; default: kernel<0><<<...>>>(...); break; } ``` But the kernel with template parameter can have `#pragma unroll` on the ic loop. Let's do that to get better performance. Similarly, we can template on kH, kW, pH, pW if we want. But those are small loops (3 and 7). Unrolling them can help a lot because it allows the compiler to schedule loads and FMAs better, and possibly keep `row_ptr` and `gw` calculations in registers. Let's template the kernel on `C`, `kH`, `kW`, `pH`, `pW`. ```cuda template <int C, int kH, int kW, int pH, int pW> __global__ void conv2d_same_kernel(...) { ... } ``` In the kernel, we write: ```cuda #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < C; ++ic) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; ++kh) { int gh = h + kh - pH; bool h_valid = (gh >= 0 && gh < H); const float* row_ptr = input + ((n * C + ic) * H + gh) * W; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < kW; ++kw) { int gw = w + kw - pW; float in_val = 0.0f; if (h_valid && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { in_val = row_ptr[gw]; } float w_val = weight[((oc * C + ic) * kH + kh) * kW + kw]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } } ``` Since `C=32`, `kH=3`, `kW=7`, total unrolled iterations = 672. The compiler might generate a large amount of code but should handle it. The kernel binary size might be large, but 672 FMAs unrolled is okay. But there's a caveat: Unrolling large loops with loads can increase register pressure if the compiler keeps multiple loads in flight. However, each load is to a different address, but the compiler can pipeline them. Actually, unrolling 32*3*7=672 iterations might increase instruction cache pressure. The kernel code size could be large (~ a few KB). That's fine. Alternatively, we can unroll only kh and kw loops, and keep ic loop rolled. That reduces code size while still optimizing the inner part. Let's do `#pragma unroll` on `kh` and `kw` only. The `ic` loop can be rolled or unrolled partially. Let's try unrolling all first and see if it compiles. But wait: `h_valid` is computed per kh and depends on `h`. For interior h=1..510, `h_valid` is true for all kh. For edge h=0, kh=0 gives gh=-1 (false), kh=1 gives gh=0 (true), kh=2 gives gh=1 (true). The compiler doesn't know `h` at compile time. The branch `if (h_valid && ...)` will be predicated. Also, the weight address `((oc * C + ic) * kH + kh) * kW + kw` can be simplified. Since `kH` and `kW` are template parameters, the compiler can do strength reduction. But we can also precompute a base pointer for each (oc, ic): `const float* w_ptr = weight + (oc * C + ic) * kH * kW;` and then access `w_ptr[kh*kW + kw]`. Since `kh` and `kw` are unrolled, the compiler will compute constant offsets. Good. Let's refine the kernel: ```cuda template <int C, int kH, int kW, int pH, int pW> __global__ void conv2d_same_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int H, int W, int K ) { int w = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int oc = threadIdx.y; int h = blockIdx.y; int n = blockIdx.z; if (w >= W) return; float acc = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < C; ++ic) { const float* in_ptr = input + ((n * C + ic) * H + h) * W; const float* w_ptr = weight + ((oc * C + ic) * kH) * kW; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; ++kh) { int gh = h + kh - pH; bool h_valid = (gh >= 0 && gh < H); const float* row_ptr = in_ptr + (kh - pH) * W; // but careful: gh used for bounds // Actually better: row_ptr = input + ((n*C+ic)*H + gh)*W; // Let's compute directly. const float* row_ptr = input + ((n * C + ic) * H + gh) * W; // Wait, gh might be OOB. If OOB, row_ptr is invalid. But we only dereference if h_valid. #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < kW; ++kw) { int gw = w + kw - pW; float in_val = 0.0f; if (h_valid && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { in_val = row_ptr[gw]; } acc += in_val * w_ptr[kh * kW + kw]; } } } if (bias != nullptr) { acc += bias[oc]; } output[((n * K + oc) * H + h) * W + w] = acc; } ``` Wait, `row_ptr` when `gh` is OOB is an invalid pointer. But we only dereference when `h_valid` is true, so it's safe. However, computing `row_ptr` with `gh` out of bounds might overflow the pointer arithmetic if `gh` is very large negative. But `gh` is at most `-1` (since pH=1, h=0, kh=0). `((n*C+ic)*H + (-1)) * W` could underflow? In C++, pointer arithmetic with negative index on array is UB if the result is not within the array or one-past-end. `input` is a pointer to the start of the allocation. Adding a negative value is not allowed. So we must avoid computing `row_ptr` when `gh` is OOB. Better: ```cuda for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; ++kh) { int gh = h + kh - pH; if (gh >= 0 && gh < H) { const float* row_ptr = input + ((n * C + ic) * H + gh) * W; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < kW; ++kw) { int gw = w + kw - pW; float in_val = 0.0f; if (gw >= 0 && gw < W) { in_val = row_ptr[gw]; } acc += in_val * w_ptr[kh * kW + kw]; } } else { // h is out of bounds, in_val is 0. We still need to load weights? No, because in_val=0, product is 0. // But we need to not load weight? Actually, we can just skip the kw loop. // However, to keep memory access patterns consistent, we might still load weights if the compiler didn't optimize. // Since `gh` is uniform across the warp, this branch causes no divergence (all threads in block take same path). // But blocks on edge rows (h=0 or h=H-1) will take this branch for some kh. // Since the block has only one h, all threads have same `h_valid`. So the branch is uniform. } } ``` This avoids invalid pointer arithmetic. But the `else` branch does nothing. The compiler might optimize the `if (gh >= 0 && gh < H)` by predicating the inner loop. Since `gh` is uniform across the warp/block, it's fine. However, if we have `if (h_valid)` around the `row_ptr` assignment, we repeat it for each `ic`. That's okay. Alternatively, we can precompute an array of valid row pointers for the 3 kh values, using a conditional expression that avoids OOB pointer arithmetic: ```cuda const float* in_base = input + ((n * C + ic) * H); for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; ++kh) { int gh = h + kh - pH; const float* row_ptr = (gh >= 0 && gh < H) ? (in_base + gh * W) : nullptr; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < kW; ++kw) { int gw = w + kw - pW; float in_val = 0.0f; if (row_ptr != nullptr && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { in_val = row_ptr[gw]; } acc += in_val * w_ptr[ ... ]; } } ``` Using `nullptr` ensures no UB. The ternary operator avoids evaluating the pointer when invalid. This is safe. But now we have `row_ptr != nullptr && gw >= 0 && gw < W` inside the inner loop. The compiler can optimize `row_ptr != nullptr` as it's uniform. And `gw` bounds check is per thread (predicated). Let's keep it simple. Actually, since `h_valid` is uniform across the block, we can branch at block level: compute if the block is on top/bottom edge and adjust the kh loop bounds accordingly. But that's more complex. Given that h edges are rare (2 out of 512), the overhead is negligible. What about w edges? The `gw` check is per thread. For interior w (which is most), `gw` is always valid. For edge w (w=0,1,2 and w=1021,1022,1023), some kw are invalid. Since threadIdx.x varies, the `gw` check is per thread. The compiler will predicate the loads. The overhead is small. **Handling w >= W:** Since grid matches exactly for W=1024, tile_w=32, we can skip the check to save a register/instruction. But for generality, include `if (w >= W) return;`. **Template instantiation** We need a wrapper function that extracts dimensions and launches the kernel. Since we want to template on constants, we can have a switch on C. But since C can vary, we can write: ```cpp void dispatch(int C, int kH, int kW, int pH, int pW, ...) { dim3 block(32, C); // Wait, C is in_channels, not out_channels. K is out_channels. // Actually, we want blockDim.y = K (out_channels). In our case K=32. } ``` Our kernel uses `C` (in_channels) as a template for the ic loop. It uses `K` (out_channels) as blockDim.y and for determining threadIdx.y range. In the test, C=32, K=32. If we want to template on C, kH, kW, pH, pW, we need to dispatch. But dispatching on C requires a switch. C is at most maybe 64 or 128. A switch on C is okay. But since we don't know the exact values, and the kernel can run with dynamic C too (just slower), we can skip templating and rely on dynamic loops. Let's start with dynamic and see if it's fast enough. If not, we can hardcode. Actually, let's keep it dynamic for flexibility. I need to write a proper CUDA kernel for ConvTranspose2d. The parameters are: - batch_size = 8 - in_channels = 32 - out_channels = 32 - kernel_size = (3, 7) - height = 512 - width = 1024 - stride = (1, 1) - padding = (1, 3) When stride=1, ConvTranspose2d is equivalent to Conv2d if we flip the weights appropriately and set padding differently. Actually, for stride=1, ConvTranspose2d is equivalent to an ordinary convolution applied to a padded input with possibly a different weight configuration. More specifically, `nn.ConvTranspose2d(in, out, kernel, stride=1, padding=p)` is equivalent to `nn.Conv2d(in, out, kernel, stride=1, padding=kernel-1-p)` applied to the same input, but with the weight tensor flipped both horizontally and vertically and the in/out channel dimensions swapped. Wait, let me be precise. The PyTorch documentation says: For stride=1, ConvTranspose2d is equivalent to Conv2d with `padding = kernel_size - 1 - padding` and `weight = weight.flip(2,3).transpose(0,1)` (or something along those lines). Actually: `ConvTranspose2d(in, out, k, stride=1, padding=p)` is equivalent to `Conv2d(in, out, k, stride=1, padding=k-1-p)` applied to the same input with the weight tensor `W` replaced by `W.flip(2,3).permute(1,0,2,3)`? No, the in/out dims need to be swapped. The ConvTranspose2d weight has shape (in, out, kH, kW). A Conv2d weight would have shape (out, in, kH, kW). So we need to permute the first two axes. Specifically: - ConvTranspose2d weight W: (in_channels, out_channels, kH, kW) - Equivalent Conv2d weight W': (out_channels, in_channels, kH, kW) - W' = W.flip(2,3).permute(1, 0, 2, 3) or equivalently W.flip(2,3).transpose(0,1) And the equivalent padding is: `padding = (kH - 1 - pH, kW - 1 - pW)`. Given kH=3, kW=7, pH=1, pW=3. Equivalent padding = (3 - 1 - 1, 7 - 1 - 3) = (1, 3). Wait, that's the same padding! Let me verify: `ConvTranspose2d(padding=1,3)` with kernel 3,7. If `padding = kernel_size - 1 - original_padding`. original_padding for transpose is backward padding... Let me recall: For Conv2d: output_size = floor((input + 2*padding - kernel)/stride) + 1 For ConvTranspose2d: output_size = (input - 1)*stride - 2*padding + kernel + output_padding With stride=1: output_size = input - 2*padding + kernel So if we want the same output size as input (which we do when padding=1 for kernel=3, stride=1): output_size = 512 - 2*1 + 3 = 512. Good. output_size = 1024 - 2*3 + 7 = 1024. Good. For a regular Conv2d with stride=1, output_size = input + 2*padding - kernel + 1. To get output_size = input: padding = (kernel - 1) / 2. For kernel=3, padding=1 gives output=input. For kernel=7, padding=3 gives output=input. So the transposed convolution with stride=1 and padding=(1,3), kernel=(3,7) DOES produce the same spatial dimensions as the input. Now, the equivalent conv2d for a transposed conv is: `conv2d(x, weight.flip(2,3).permute(1,0,2,3), padding=(kH-1-pH, kW-1-pW))` Wait, no. Let's derive it properly. The gradient of Conv2d with respect to its input is a ConvTranspose2d with the same weight (possibly transposed and flipped). So if `y = Conv2d(x, W)`, then `grad_x = ConvTranspose2d(grad_y, W, padding=padding)`. This means `ConvTranspose2d(z, W, padding) = Conv2d(z, W', padding')`? Not quite. But PyTorch has `grad.conv2d_input` which computes the gradient. Actually there's a theorem: `ConvTranspose2d(x, W, stride, padding)` is equivalent to `Conv2d(x, W_rotated, padding=kernel-1-padding)` where `W_rotated` is `W` with spatial dims flipped and in/out channels transposed? Let's check a reference. According to: "Is ConvTranspose2d+stride=1 equivalent to Conv2d?" on PyTorch forums: For stride=1: `ConvTranspose2d(in, out, k, stride=1, padding=p)` is equivalent to `Conv2d(out, in, k, stride=1, padding=k-1-p)` applied to input x with weight `weight.transpose(0,1).flip(2,3)`. Wait, if the ConvTranspose2d takes input of shape (N, in) and outputs (N, out), then the equivalent Conv2d must take (N, in) and output (N, out). So the weight should have shape (out, in, k, k). The original ConvTranspose2d weight has shape (in, out, k, k). So yes, transpose(0,1). Specifically: `y = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in, out, kH, kW, stride=1, padding=p)(x)` is equivalent to `y = nn.Conv2d(in, out, kH, kW, stride=1, padding=kH-1-p, bias=bias)(x)` with weight `weight = conv_transpose.weight.transpose(0,1).flip(2,3)`. And if `bias` is present, it's the same bias. Let me test this mathematically. Consider 1D for simplicity. Let x be length L, weight w be length k, padding p. Conv1d output y[n] = sum_{m=0}^{k-1} w[m] * x[n + m - p] (with appropriate bounds). ConvTranspose1d output z[n] = sum_{m=0}^{k-1} w[m] * x[n - m + p] = sum_{m=0}^{k-1} w[k-1-m'] * x[n - (k-1-m') + p] = sum_{m'=0}^{k-1} w_flipped[m'] * x[n + m' - (k-1-p)] This is exactly Conv1d with weight flipped and padding = k-1-p. The channel dimensions: ConvTranspose1d weight is (in, out, k). Conv1d weight is (out, in, k). So we swap those too. Wait in my derivation above, z[n] = sum_m w[m] * x[n - m + p]. Let u = k-1-m => m = k-1-u. z[n] = sum_{u=0}^{k-1} w[k-1-u] * x[n - (k-1-u) + p] = sum_{u=0}^{k-1} w_flipped[u] * x[n + u - (k-1-p)] Yes! So it's a conv1d with w_flipped and padding = k-1-p. So for 2D: Equivalent conv2d uses: - weight = conv_transpose.weight.transpose(0,1).flip(2,3) - padding = (kH-1-pH, kW-1-pW) - bias = conv_transpose.bias (same) In our case: kH=3, pH=1 => equiv padding H = 3-1-1 = 1 kW=7, pW=3 => equiv padding W = 7-1-3 = 3 So the equivalent Conv2d padding is (1, 3) which is the SAME as the given padding. Interesting. This means `ConvTranspose2d(IC, OC, (3,7), stride=1, padding=(1,3))` is equivalent to `Conv2d(IC, OC, (3,7), stride=1, padding=(1,3))` with `weight = transpose(IC,OC).flip(2,3)`. This is crucial. So I can just call `F.conv2d(x, self.weight.transpose(0,1).flip(2,3), self.bias, padding=(1,3))`. But the task is to write a faster custom CUDA kernel. Let me try a well-structured direct conv kernel: Given OC=32, IC=32, KH=3, KW=7. This is small enough that we can load all weights into shared memory or even registers for a block of threads. Weight size: 32*32*3*7 = 21,504 floats = 86KB. For an SM with 64K registers (actually 64K 32-bit per thread block, but max 255 per thread, so 65,280 per block of 256 threads). 86KB is too large for registers if all threads keep a copy. But if we load it into shared memory once per block, all threads can access it. With 86KB of weight in shared memory, plus input tile, we might exceed 100KB per block, which is fine (limit 227KB). Input tile: let's pick a spatial tile of (8, 32). For output tile (8, 32), input tile with halo is (8+2, 32+6) = (10, 38). 10*38 = 380 pixels per channel. For 32 channels = 12,160 pixels. 12,160 * 4 bytes = 48,640 bytes. Total shared memory: 86,016 + 48,640 = 134,656 bytes ≈ 131.5 KB. This fits comfortably. With 131.5 KB per block, max 1 block per SM on some arch, but on H100 max 227KB, so 1 block per SM. Occupancy = 1 * 256 threads = 256 threads per SM. H100 has 132 SMs. Total resident threads = 33,792. GPU can run many more, but this is fine if the kernel is efficient. Block configuration: - Threads per block: 256. - Output tile: 8x32 pixels, 32 output channels. - Total output elements per block: 8*32*32 = 8,192. - Each thread computes 8192/256 = 32 output values. - So each thread computes 32 values. This can be structured as 1 pixel for 32 channels, or 4 pixels for 8 channels, etc. Best: each thread computes 1 output spatial pixel for all 32 output channels. Then we need 8*32 = 256 threads. Yes! - Thread (ty, tx) where ty in [0,7), tx in [0,32). - Thread (ty, tx) computes output at (out_h = block_h*8 + ty, out_w = block_w*32 + tx) for all 32 output channels. - So each thread computes 32 output values (1 per channel). Loading weights into shared memory: All 256 threads cooperate to load weight into shared memory. Weight shape: (OC, IC, KH, KW) = (32, 32, 3, 7) floats. Each thread loads: 21504 / 256 = 84 floats (plus a few extra for the last thread). That's doable. Actually we can just have each thread load a contiguous chunk: size_t weight_offset = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; // 0..255 for (idx = weight_offset; idx < 21504; idx += 256) s_weight[idx] = weight[idx]; Loading input into shared memory: The input tile for output block is: h_start = blockIdx.y * 8 w_start = blockIdx.x * 32 The input tile covers h_start to h_start+8+2 (since KH=3, pH=1 for the equivalent conv2d), and w_start to w_start+32+6 (KW=7, pW=3). Wait, for the equivalent conv2d with padding=(1,3): Input tile needed for output (h, w) is input at (h+pH-0..2, w+pW-0..6) relative to block. Actually for regular conv2d, to compute output at (h,w): input needed is from (h-pH to h-pH+KH-1) = (h-1 to h+1) for H dimension? No. Standard conv2d with padding p: we conceptually pad the input. The "valid" input region contributing to output (h,w) is the original input at (h + kh - p) for kh in 0..KH-1. So for h = block_h*8 + ty, the input rows are [block_h*8 + ty - 1, block_h*8 + ty + 1]. The union over ty=0..7 is [block_h*8 - 1, block_h*8 + 8]. So the input tile height is 8 + 2 = 10 (rows: -1 to 8 relative to block start). Similarly for W: union over tx=0..31 is [block_w*32 - 3, block_w*32 + 38]. So the input tile width is 32 + 6 = 38. Total input tile: 10 rows x 38 cols x 32 channels = 12,160 floats. Each thread loads part of the input tile. For channel ic, row ih in [0, 9], col iw in [0, 37]: global_h = block_h*8 + ih - 1 global_w = block_w*32 + iw - 3 If global_h in [0, H) and global_w in [0, W), load input[n, ic, global_h, global_w], else load 0. We can have each thread load a chunk of the input tile. 12,160 floats / 256 threads = 47.5 floats per thread. Very manageable. Algorithm: 1. Load weight (32*32*3*7 floats) into shared memory `s_weight`. 2. For each batch n and output channel block... wait, our block computes ALL 32 output channels. So grid should be: (W/32, H/8, N). Each block processes one batch item. Block computes 8x32 output spatial locations for ALL 32 output channels. 3. Load input tile for this block. Input tile is 10x38x32 = 12,160 values. 4. __syncthreads(). 5. Each thread computes 32 output values: For oc = 0..31: acc[oc] = bias[oc] (if any) For ic = 0..31: For kh = 0..2: For kw = 0..6: in_val = s_input[kh_offset + ty + something, kw_offset + tx + something, ic] for oc = 0..31: acc[oc] += in_val * s_weight[oc, ic, kh, kw] But wait, for each spatial output point, the input is different. Let's formalize. For thread (ty, tx), output spatial point is (h = bh*8 + ty, w = bw*32 + tx). The input (after padding) at (h - 1 + kh, w - 3 + kw) for kh,kw. In shared memory, we stored padded input tile. Let ih = ty + kh? No. The output h = bh*8 + ty uses input row h - 1 + kh = bh*8 + ty - 1 + kh. In shared memory, we stored rows relative to bh*8: row 0 corresponds to bh*8 - 1. So the shared memory row index is (ty - 1 + kh) + 1 = ty + kh. Wait, if s_input row 0 is bh*8 - 1: Then global row bh*8 - 1 + r corresponds to s_input row r. We need global row bh*8 + ty - 1 + kh. So s_input row = (bh*8 + ty - 1 + kh) - (bh*8 - 1) = ty + kh. Similarly for col: s_input col 0 corresponds to global col bw*32 - 3. We need global col bw*32 + tx - 3 + kw. s_input col = (bw*32 + tx - 3 + kw) - (bw*32 - 3) = tx + kw. Perfect! So for thread (ty, tx): in_val = s_input[ic, ty + kh, tx + kw] where s_input has shape (32, 10, 38). But we need to handle boundaries. For h=0, ty=0, kh=0: s_input row = 0. This corresponds to global row -1, which should be 0 (padding). We should have loaded 0 for out-of-bounds. Similarly for w: if tx=0, kw=0: s_input col=0 -> global col bw*32 - 3. If bw=0, this is -3 -> 0 (padding). Our cooperative load should have already placed 0 for out-of-bounds. So the kernel is: ``` extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* s_weight = smem; // size: OC * IC * KH * KW float* s_input = smem + OC*IC*KH*KW; // size: IC * (TILE_H+KH-1) * (TILE_W+KW-1) // load weight int tid = threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; for (int i = tid; i < OC*IC*KH*KW; i += blockDim.x*blockDim.y) { s_weight[i] = weight[i]; } // load input int TILE_H = 8, TILE_W = 32; int IH_TILE = TILE_H + KH - 1; // 10 int IW_TILE = TILE_W + KW - 1; // 38 int IN_TILE_SIZE = IC * IH_TILE * IW_TILE; for (int i = tid; i < IN_TILE_SIZE; i += blockDim.x*blockDim.y) { int ic = i / (IH_TILE * IW_TILE); int rem = i % (IH_TILE * IW_TILE); int ih = rem / IW_TILE; int iw = rem % IW_TILE; int gh = blockIdx.y * TILE_H + ih - pH; // pH=1 int gw = blockIdx.x * TILE_W + iw - pW; // pW=3 float val = 0.0f; if (gh >= 0 && gh < H && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { val = input[n, ic, gh, gw]; } s_input[i] = val; } __syncthreads(); // compute float acc[OC]; // OC=32, unroll? // initialize from bias if (bias) { for (int oc = 0; oc < OC; ++oc) acc[oc] = bias[oc]; } else { for (int oc = 0; oc < OC; ++oc) acc[oc] = 0.0f; } int ty = threadIdx.y; int tx = threadIdx.x; for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ++ic) { for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { float in_val = s_input[ic * IH_TILE * IW_TILE + (ty + kh) * IW_TILE + (tx + kw)]; // Unfortunately KW=7 might not fully unroll, but KH=3 can be unrolled. #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < OC; ++oc) { acc[oc] += in_val * s_weight[oc * IC * KH * KW + ic * KH * KW + kh * KW + kw]; } } } } // store output for (int oc = 0; oc < OC; ++oc) { output[n, oc, h, w] = acc[oc]; } ``` Wait, this has an inner loop over OC=32. For each input pixel and weight pixel, we do 32 FMAs. Total inner loop iterations per thread: IC(32) * KH(3) * KW(7) * OC(32) = 21,504 FMAs. At 67 TFLOPS, that's 21,504 * 2 / 67e12 per thread = ~0.6 microseconds per thread if fully compute bound. Total thread count: (W/32)*(H/8)*N = (1024/32)*(512/8)*8 = 32*64*8 = 16,384 blocks. Each block has 256 threads. Total threads launched = 16,384 * 256 = 4,194,304. Total FLOPs = 4,194,304 * 21,504 * 2 = 180 billion? Wait. Actually output elements = N * OC * H * W = 8 * 32 * 512 * 1024 = 134,217,728. Each output element requires IC*KH*KW = 672 multiplications and 671 additions. So total MACs = 134M * 672 ≈ 90.2 GFLOPs (multiply-adds, so 180.4 GFLOP operations). At 67 TFLOPS FP32, minimum time = 180.4 / 67,000 ≈ 2.69 ms. But Tensor Cores can do much more. And actual throughput might be lower. Wait, each thread computes 32 output elements (1 for each OC). So each thread does 32 * 672 = 21,504 MACs. Total threads = 512*1024/256 * 8 = 16,384. 16,384 * 21,504 = 352.3M MACs? That doesn't match 90.2G. Ah, because IC=32, KH=3, KW=7. Each output (spatial, channel) needs 672 MACs. But each thread computes 32 outputs (one per OC), so 32*672 = 21,504 MACs per thread. Number of spatial points = 512*1024*8 = 4,194,304. These are distributed among threads. Wait, my block computes an 8x32 spatial tile, so 256 spatial points per block. Each thread handles 1 spatial point but all 32 channels. So 16,384 blocks * 256 spatial points = 4,194,304 spatial points. OK. MACs = 4,194,304 spatial * 32 channels * 672 = 90.2G. Yes. Per thread: 21,504 MACs. With FP32 peak 67 TFLOPS, perthread time doesn't matter, total matters. Is this kernel fast enough? The inner loops are: - `ic` (32) - `kh` (3) -> can be unrolled - `kw` (7) -> can be unrolled - `oc` (32) -> can be unrolled (at least partial) But unrolling all 32 OC with 32 floats in registers might lead to register pressure. 32 accumulators + maybe some temporaries. For H100, max 255 registers per thread. 32 float accumulators = 32 registers. That leaves 223 for other things. lw and other temps. This is fine. However, the compiler might not keep all 32 values in registers and might spill if we're not careful. To help, I can process OC in chunks, e.g., 8 at a time, doing 4 passes. Or each thread can compute a subset of OC. Alternative: each thread computes 1 output point for 4 output channels. Then block computes 8x32 spatial, 4 channels per thread -> but we need to cover 32 channels. With 256 threads, if each thread does 4 channels, total channel capacity = 256*4 = 1024, which is much more than 32. So we can have threads cooperate on channels. Better: each block computes 8x32 spatial for ALL 32 channels. We can have 32 warps, each warp handles 1 output channel? No, 256 threads = 8 warps. Each warp handles 32/8 = 4 output channels. So warp 0 handles oc=0..3, warp 1 handles oc=4..7, etc. Within a warp, 32 threads each compute 1 spatial point for their 4 channels. This is a lot more synchronized at the warp level and might reduce register pressure. But let's try the simpler approach first: each thread has 32 accumulators and compute all 32 channels. We can also reverse the loop order for better memory access: Currently accessing `s_weight[oc * stride ...]` for varying oc. If we iterate `oc` in the inner loop, we access s_weight with a stride of IC*KH*KW (which is 672 floats = 2688 bytes). That's a big stride. Not good for caching. The L1 cache line is 128 bytes. Each access loads 128 bytes (32 floats). So stride of 2688 bytes means each access goes to a different cache line. For 32 channels, that's 32 cache lines per (ic, kh, kw) iteration. 32 cache lines * 128 bytes = 4096 bytes. In one iteration of the innermost `kw` loop (with ic fixed), we access 32 cache lines of weight. Then for next kw, we access 32 more cache lines. Then next kh, etc. This might still be OK in L1 if the cache is large enough (128KB L1 per SM on H100). 32 cache lines * 128 bytes = 4KB. For 7 kw values * 3 kh values = 21 iterations, total weight for one ic is 32 * 21 * 128 = 86KB. Actually the whole weight for one ic across all oc is 32*3*7 = 672 floats per oc, total 21,504 floats = 86KB. So the entire weight fits in L1! (L1 is 128KB per SM on H100). Great. And the input per ic is 10*38 = 380 floats = 1.5KB. So for one ic, we need to access 86KB of weight and 1.5KB of input. The input for the next ic is adjacent in memory (if we store s_input as [ic, ih, iw]). But note: our s_input is in shared memory, and s_weight is in shared memory too. Shared memory access latency is low. And banks are 32-bit, 32 banks. If we access s_weight for different oc but same (ic, kh, kw), the bank access pattern depends on the layout. If s_weight is [oc][ic][kh][kw], then for fixed ic,kh,kw, s_weight[oc*stride + offset] where stride = IC*KH*KW = 672. 672 * 4 bytes = 2688 bytes. 2688 / 4 = 672 banks away. 672 % 32 = 16. So thread with oc=0 accesses bank 16 (assuming offset aligns to bank 0). Thread with oc=1 accesses bank (16+672)%32 = ... 672%32 = 16. So all threads access the same bank! Bank conflict! This is bad. Since 32 threads in a warp access different oc but the stride is 672 elements = 16 banks, they all map to the same bank? Wait: For thread tx in warp, accessing s_weight[oc * 672 + same_offset] where oc = tx. Bank = (base_addr + tx*672*4) / 4 % 32 = (base_addr/4 + tx*672) % 32. Since 672 % 32 = 16. Bank = (base + tx*16) % 32. For tx=0..31: banks are base, base+16, base+0, base+16, ... (since 16*2=32). So threads with even tx access bank base, threads with odd tx access bank base+16. This means 16 threads access bank A and 16 threads access bank B -> 2-way bank conflict (actually 16-way? No, 16 threads to 1 bank means serialized in 16 groups if each bank can handle 1 request per cycle, or 2 cycles per warp? Actually for 2-way conflict (2 distinct banks), it takes 2 transactions. 16-way would be 16 transactions. Here we have 2 distinct banks, so 2 transactions per warp. That's acceptable! Wait, let me recalculate. Address = base + tx * (672 * 4) Offset in bytes = tx * 2688 Bank = (offset / 4) % 32 = (tx * 672) % 32. 672 = 32*21 + 0. Wait, 32*21 = 672. So 672 % 32 = 0! Ah! I made a mistake. 32 * 21 = 672. Exactly! So 672 % 32 = 0. Bank = (tx * 0) % 32 = 0 for all tx! This means ALL threads in a warp access bank 0 when reading s_weight with varying oc and fixed (ic,kh,kw). This is a 32-way bank conflict! DISASTER! So we absolutely cannot store weight as [oc][ic][kh][kw] if we iterate oc in the inner loop. How to fix? We should store weight as [ic][kh][kw][oc] or some permutation where the inner dimension is oc and contiguous. If we store s_weight with layout [ic][kh][kw][oc], then for fixed ic,kh,kw, the addresses of oc are consecutive: base + oc*4. Then thread tx accesses base + tx*4. Bank = (base_offset + tx) % 32. For tx=0..31, banks are base, base+1, ..., base+31 (mod 32) — NO BANK CONFLICT! Perfect. So I should load weight into shared memory in transposed order: [ic][kh][kw][oc]. But the original weight tensor is [oc][ic][kh][kw]. So during loading, each thread does: ``` int idx = tid; int oc = idx / (IC*KH*KW); int rem = idx % (IC*KH*KW); int ic = rem / (KH*KW); int rem2 = rem % (KH*KW); int kh = rem2 / KW; int kw = rem2 % KW; s_weight[(((ic * KH + kh) * KW + kw) * OC + oc)] = weight[(((oc * IC + ic) * KH + kh) * KW + kw)]; ``` Similarly for input, if we store as [ic][ih][iw], and threads access [ic][ty+kh][tx+kw], for fixed ic, ty, tx, varying kh and kw. For kw varying, address is base + kw. Banks = (base_offset + kw) % 32. 32 threads in a warp have the same ty, tx? No! tx varies across the warp. Inside a warp, tx ranges over 32 consecutive values. So for a fixed kh, threads access s_input[ic][ty+kh][tx+kw] for tx=0..31 and some fixed kw. Address = base + (ty+kh)*IW_TILE + (tx+kw). Bank = (address_offset + tx + kw) % 32. Since tx covers 0..31, and kw is the same for all threads in the inner kw loop, the banks are consecutive (after adding kw). No bank conflict for varying tx. But what if blockDim.x = 32 and threadIdx.x is tx? Yes. Then any fixed kw, varying tx is bank-conflict-free. What about varying kh? The inner loop is over kh, then kw. For each (kh, kw), all threads load one input value. Input address depends on (ty+kh)*IW_TILE + (tx+kw). IW_TILE = 38. 38 % 32 = 6. So for two different kh values: Address1: base + ty*38 + tx + kh1*38 + kw Bank1: (offset + ty*38 + tx + kh1*38 + kw) % 32 Since tx varies 0..31, the banks are offset by tx, which covers all banks. No conflict. For adjacent kh, the row offset is 38. 38 % 32 = 6. So the bank shifts by 6. Still covers all banks for varying tx. No problem. So INPUT access is fine with [ic][ih][iw] layout. WEIGHT access requires [ic][kh][kw][oc] layout. What about the inner loop order? We want: ``` for ic in 0..IC-1: for kh in 0..KH-1: for kw in 0..KW-1: float in_val = s_input[ic, ty+kh, tx+kw]; #pragma unroll for oc_chunk in 0..(OC/OC_UNROLL-1): float w0 = s_weight[ic, kh, kw, oc_chunk*OC_UNROLL + 0]; ... acc[oc_chunk*OC_UNROLL + 0] += in_val * w0; } ``` If OC=32, and we unroll by 8 (doing 4 chunks), each chunk loads 8 contiguous floats. 8 contiguous floats = 32 bytes = 2 128-bit cache lines or 8 banks. 32 threads load 8 floats each? No, each thread loads 1 float per oc iteration. Wait, let's restructure. If each thread computes ALL 32 output channels, then in the inner `oc` loop, thread 0 accesses oc=0, thread 1 accesses... actually it's not a parallel loop, it's a sequential loop within each thread. Each thread computes its own 32 output channels independently. So there is no parallel access to s_weight for varying oc. Just one thread accessing oc=0, then oc=1, etc. sequentially. This is fine! No bank conflicts for parallel access because there is only one thread accessing the weight for its own accumulators. BUT: the warp executes all threads in lockstep (in SIMT). They are all in the same `oc` loop iteration at the same time. So when all threads are on `oc=0`, they all access `s_weight[ic, kh, kw, 0]`. That's the SAME address for all threads in the warp. Broadcast! No bank conflict. When they all move to `oc=1`, they all access `s_weight[ic, kh, kw, 1]`. Same address. Broadcast! This is PERFECT! Because the oc loop is the same for all threads, all threads access the same weight element simultaneously. Shared memory broadcasts this to all threads in one cycle. No bank conflict at all! Wait, are the threads accessing the same address? Yes! In the oc loop, `s_weight[ic, kh, kw, oc]`, and `oc` is the loop variable, same for all threads. Address doesn't depend on threadIdx.x or threadIdx.y. So YES, perfect broadcast! But what about the input value? `s_input[ic, ty+kh, tx+kw]`. Here `tx` varies across threads. So threads access different columns, which are consecutive in memory since IW_TILE = 38. For a warp of 32 threads, tx covers 32 consecutive values (assuming blockDim.x=32). Are there bank conflicts? Address = base + (ty+kh)*38 + (tx+kw). For tx=0..31: addresses are base', base'+1, ..., base'+31. These map to 32 different banks (bank = address / 4 % 32). Since addresses differ by 1, banks differ by 1. No bank conflict! (Except maybe at boundaries of 32, but 32 consecutive addresses cover all 32 banks exactly once). Even though IW_TILE=38 is not a multiple of 32, for a single warp with tx=0..31, the access is fully coalesced and bank-conflict-free. This is great! So the layout should be: s_input: [IC][IH_TILE][IW_TILE], row-major. s_weight: [IC][KH][KW][OC] (or actually for the inner loop, just [IC][KH][KW][OC] since oc is the varying dimension for broadcast access when all threads are in the same iteration). Wait, if s_weight is [IC][KH][KW][OC], then for fixed ic,kh,kw, and all threads on same oc, address is the same. Broadcast. If s_weight were [OC][IC][KH][KW], address for fixed ic,kh,kw would be oc*IC*KH*KW*stride. Still the same for all threads on same oc. Broadcast also works! Because broadcast doesn't depend on the address being the same bank, it depends on all threads in a warp accessing the same address. Since they all have the same `ic, kh, kw, oc`, the address is the same regardless of layout. So ANY layout works fine for weight when iterating oc sequentially in all threads. Wait, earlier I was worried about bank conflicts only if I parallelized oc across threads. If I keep oc sequential per thread, there are no bank conflicts for weight. So the simplest layout is [OC][IC][KH][KW] (the original) or [IC][KH][KW][OC]. Both work. I'll use the original to avoid transposing during load. For input, again original layout [N][IC][H][W] can't be used directly since we need the tile. We'll load into s_input with layout [IC][IH][IW]. So the kernel becomes much simpler! Let me write the kernel now. One more optimization: since the block computes one batch item (n = blockIdx.z), I'll set gridDim.z = N. Grid: (W/32, H/8, N) -> (32, 64, 8). Block: (32, 8, 1). Shared memory size: - s_weight: 32 * 32 * 3 * 7 = 21,504 floats = 86,016 bytes. - s_input: 32 * 10 * 38 = 12,160 floats = 48,640 bytes. Total = 134,656 bytes. We should pass this dynamically: `kernel<<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>` Wait, for height dimension: H=512. TILE_H=8. 512/8=64. So grid.y=64. For width dimension: W=1024. TILE_W=32. 1024/32=32. So grid.x=32. Total blocks: 32 * 64 * 8 = 16,384. Kernel algorithm: 1. Compute tid = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x (0..255). 2. Load weight from global to shared: For idx from tid to 21,504 step 256: s_weight[idx] = weight_ptr[idx]; 3. Load input from global to shared: For idx from tid to 12,160 step 256: Unpack idx into ic, ih, iw. Compute global coordinates gh, gw. Get val from input or 0. s_input[idx] = val. 4. __syncthreads(); 5. Compute: int tx = threadIdx.x; // 0..31 int ty = threadIdx.y; // 0..7 int h = blockIdx.y * 8 + ty; int w = blockIdx.x * 32 + tx; int n = blockIdx.z; float acc[32]; if (bias_ptr) { #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 32; ++oc) acc[oc] = bias_ptr[oc]; } else { #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 32; ++oc) acc[oc] = 0.0f; } for (int ic = 0; ic < 32; ++ic) { for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; ++kh) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 7; ++kw) { float in_val = s_input[(ic * 10 + (ty + kh)) * 38 + (tx + kw)]; // Broadcast doesn't depend on layout for weight int w_base = ((oc * 32 + ic) * 3 + kh) * 7 + kw; // oc is loop var // Wait, in inner loop acc is per oc. We need weight for each oc. // Since all threads get same (ic,kh,kw), we can load weight into register temporaries. float w0 = s_weight[ ((0 * 32 + ic) * 3 + kh) * 7 + kw ]; float w1 = s_weight[ ((1 * 32 + ic) * 3 + kh) * 7 + kw ]; ... // But this requires unrolling manually or trusting the compiler. // Actually the #pragma unroll will do this if we write: #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 32; ++oc) { acc[oc] += in_val * s_weight[ ((oc * 32 + ic) * 3 + kh) * 7 + kw ]; } } } } for (int oc = 0; oc < 32; ++oc) { output[(((n * 32 + oc) * 512 + h) * 1024 + w)] = acc[oc]; } Wait, will the compiler unroll the 32-iteration loop? Maybe. The `kh` (3) and `kw` (7) loops should definitely be unrolled. But 32 accumulators in registers... with 32*8=256 threads, 32 registers per thread for accumulators = 8192 registers per block. Draft kernel: ```cpp template<int C, int K, int KH, int KW, int pH, int pW> __global__ void conv2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int H, int W) { const int TILE_H = 8; const int TILE_W = 32; const int IH_TILE = TILE_H + KH - 1; // 10 const int IW_TILE = TILE_W + KW - 1; // 38 const int WEIGHT_SIZE = C * K * KH * KW; // 32*32*3*7 = 21504 extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* s_weight = smem; // [C][KH][KW][K] or [K][C][KH][KW] float* s_input = smem + WEIGHT_SIZE; // [C][IH_TILE][IW_TILE] int tid = threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; // 0..255 // Load weight: original layout [K][C][KH][KW] #pragma unroll for (int i = tid; i < WEIGHT_SIZE; i += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { s_weight[i] = weight[i]; } // Load input tile int IN_TILE_SIZE = C * IH_TILE * IW_TILE; // 12160 int n = blockIdx.z; #pragma unroll for (int i = tid; i < IN_TILE_SIZE; i += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int ic = i / (IH_TILE * IW_TILE); int rem = i % (IH_TILE * IW_TILE); int ih = rem / IW_TILE; int iw = rem % IW_TILE; int gh = blockIdx.y * TILE_H + ih - pH; int gw = blockIdx.x * TILE_W + iw - pW; float val = 0.0f; if (gh >= 0 && gh < H && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { val = input[((n * C + ic) * H + gh) * W + gw]; } s_input[i] = val; } __syncthreads(); int tx = threadIdx.x; // 0..31 int ty = threadIdx.y; // 0..7 int h = blockIdx.y * TILE_H + ty; int w = blockIdx.x * TILE_W + tx; float acc[K]; // 32 accumulators if (bias != nullptr) { #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < K; ++oc) { acc[oc] = bias[oc]; } } else { #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < K; ++oc) { acc[oc] = 0.0f; } } #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < C; ++ic) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { float in_val = s_input[(ic * IH_TILE + (ty + kh)) * IW_TILE + (tx + kw)]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < K; ++oc) { acc[oc] += in_val * s_weight[((oc * C + ic) * KH + kh) * KW + kw]; } } } } #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < K; ++oc) { output[(((n * K + oc) * H + h) * W + w)] = acc[oc]; } } ``` Host wrapper: ```cpp torch::Tensor conv2d_forward(const torch::Tensor& input, const torch::Tensor& weight, const torch::Tensor& bias, int N, int C, int K, int H, int W, int kH, int kW, int pH, int pW) { auto output = torch::empty({N, K, H, W}, input.options()); const int TILE_H = 8; const int TILE_W = 32; dim3 block(TILE_W, TILE_H); dim3 grid((W + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W, (H + TILE_H - 1) / TILE_H, N); int shared_mem = C * K * kH * kW * sizeof(float) + C * (TILE_H + kH - 1) * (TILE_W + kW - 1) * sizeof(float); return output; } ``` Wait, I should check the launch parameters: W=1024, TILE_W=32 -> grid.x = 32. The register ILP from 32 accumulators should hide FMA latency. Actually, on modern GPUs, FMAs have a latency of ~4 cycles. To hide 4-cycle latency, you need 4 independent FMAs in flight. We have 32 independent accumulators, so we can issue 32 FMAs before the first one's result is needed. This is massive ILP.
